Introduction to Imdad al-Fattah: A Hanafi Fiqh Commentary
Title page and basmalah of a Hanafi fiqh commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author, title, and school orientation of Imdad al-Fattah, and explain the purpose of a sharh in the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Imdad al-Fattah is a verified Hanafi commentary on Nur al-Idah, by Shurunbulali, a key figure in later Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson opens your study of Imdad al-Fattah, a monumental commentary on the Hanafi primer Nur al-Idah. Even before diving into legal rulings, understanding the text's authorship, school affiliation, and editorial quality is essential. A sharh is not merely an explanation; it is a tool for transmitting and refining the madhhab's positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the text and author
Note the full title: Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah. The author is Hasan al-Shurunbulali (994-1069 AH). The work is explicitly according to the school of Abu Hanifa.
Abu Hanifa is the school founder, not the author.
2
Understand the editorial features
This edition is described as colored, with verified texts, authenticated hadith, and a comprehensive index. It is printed for the first time in this format.
Edition quality affects reliability.
3
Recognize the role of the basmalah
The basmalah (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم) is a traditional Islamic invocation opening any significant work. It signifies the author's reliance on Allah.
All classical fiqh works begin with the basmalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امداد الفتح شرح نور الإيضاح للعلامة الجليل حسن بن عمار بن علي الشرنبلالي ٩٩٤ - ١٠٦٩هـ عَلى مَذهَب الإمام الأعظم أبي حنيفة النَّعْمَانِ رَحِمَهُ الله تَعَالَى - طَبعَةٌ مُلوّنةٌ وَمُوثَقَةِ النُّصُوص مَعَ  ِفهْرَسَةٍ شَامِلَةٍ - يُطْبَعُ لأول مرة. حققَهُ وَخَرَّجَ أَحَادِيثَهُ وَعَلَّق عليه بشار بكري عرابي بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah by the eminent scholar Hasan ibn 'Ammar ibn 'Ali al-Shurunbulali (994-1069 AH) according to the school of the Greatest Imam Abu Hanifa al-Nu'man (may Allah have mercy on him). A colored and verified edition with a comprehensive index, printed for the first time. Edited, hadiths authenticated, and annotated by Bashar Bakri 'Arabi. In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.
Source pages p. 1

Commentary
This is the title page of Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary (sharh) on the matn Nur al-Idah, a well-known primer in Hanafi fiqh. The author is Hasan al-Shurunbulali (994-1069 AH), a prominent Hanafi jurist. The work strictly follows the Hanafi school. This edition is verified and includes an index. The basmalah is the traditional opening invoking Allah's name.

Key Terms
نور الإيضاح (Nur al-Idah): The base matn (text) being commented upon, authored by Imam al-Shurunbulali himself.
امداد الفتح (Imdad al-Fattah): The title of the commentary (sharh) on Nur al-Idah, meaning 'Aid of the Opener'.
مذهب (madhhab): School of Islamic jurisprudence; here specifically the Hanafi school.
شرح (sharh): A commentary or exposition of a base text, providing explanation, evidence, and elaboration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who is the author of Imdad al-Fattah?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Hasan al-Shurunbulali
C) Bashar Bakri 'Arabi
D) Muhammad al-Shaybani

Key Takeaway
Imdad al-Fattah is a reliable Hanafi commentary by Shurunbulali, building on his earlier matn Nur al-Idah, and this edition is verified and indexed.

The Role of Devotional Prefaces in Hanafi Fiqh Literature
Devotional praise of the Prophet Muhammad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose of a dedication (إهداء) in a classical Hanafi fiqh text and distinguish it from legal rulings.

Core Idea
A book's opening praise is not law; it frames the author's reverence and intention seeking blessings.

Many classical Hanafi fiqh works begin with a dedication to the Prophet Muhammad and his Companions. This passage from 'Imdad al-Fattah' is one such example. It is not a source of fiqh rulings but a devotional preface that sets the tone for the scholarly work that follows. Understanding this helps avoid misinterpreting poetic praises as legal proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literary genre
Recognize this passage as a dedication (إهداء) and not a legal ruling. The language is poetic and hyperbolic.
No legal terms like 'wajib' or 'haram' appear.
2
Categorize the claims
The miracles mentioned (moon splitting, lizard testimony) are hagiographical, not legal evidence. They are not cited as proofs for any fiqh ruling.
In Hanafi usul, such narratives are not considered a source of legislation.
3
Connect to Hanafi tradition
Hanafi scholars often open with such praises to seek barakah and honor the Prophet. This is a common practice across madhabs.
It reflects the author's adab (etiquette) and reverence.
4
Distinguish from fiqh content
No ruling, condition, or proof is presented. The passage is purely devotional and should be read as a preface, not as a source of ahkam.
Treat it as context, not content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشر انت الحر الحمى
الإهداء
إلى أشرف مبعوث في الأمم... إلى مَنْ بِهِ الرُّسل الكرام ختم...
إلى الممدوح في سورة طه ويس والقلم... لمن قال للبدر في السماء: ألا يــا بـدر انقسم قسمين فانقسم... وللناقة مَنْ أنا؟ قالت: أنت المُشَفَعُ في الأمم...
إلى من قال للضَّب: اشهد أني رسول الله، قال الضب: نعم...
إلى من شَقَّ جبريل صدره صغيراً ولم يَشْكُ بذلك مِنْ أَلَمْ...
إلى من سار ليلاً إلى العلى وكان جبريل لَه مِن أطْوَعِ الخَدَم...
إلى من قال: ربِّ أُمَّتِي نَجْهَا مِنَ النيران، قال الرب: نعم...
سيدنا ومولانا محمد رسول الله
وإلى أصحاب رسول الله والتابعين رضي الله عنهم أجمعين وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد سيد المرسلين
You are mankind's free protector. The dedication: To the most noble messenger among the nations... to him through whom the noble messengers were sealed... To the one praised in Surah Ta-Ha, Ya-Sin, and Al-Qalam... To him who said to the moon in the sky: 'O moon, split into two halves,' and it split... And to the she-camel [when asked] 'Who am I?' she said: 'You are the intercessor for the nations.'... To him who said to the lizard: 'Bear witness that I am the Messenger of Allah,' and the lizard said: 'Yes.'... To him whose chest Gabriel split open as a child and he complained not of pain therefrom... To him who traveled by night to the heights and Gabriel was for him the most obedient of servants... To him who said: 'My Lord, save my nation from the Fire,' and the Lord said: 'Yes.'... Our master and our patron Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah. And to the companions of the Messenger of Allah and the followers, may Allah be pleased with them all. And may Allah bless our master Muhammad, the master of the messengers.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a front matter dedication (إهداء) that opens the book. It is not a legal ruling but a devotional expression of love and reverence for the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). The author employs poetic and hyperbolic language, recounting several miracles: the splitting of the moon, the she-camel's testimony, the lizard's testimony, the splitting of the Prophet's chest as a child, the Isra and Mi'raj, and the intercession for the Ummah on the Day of Judgment. The dedication extends to the Companions and the Tabi'in. In the Hanafi tradition, such dedications are customary in fiqh books to invoke blessings and emphasize the centrality of the Prophet in Islamic teachings.

Key Terms
الإهداء (al-ihdā’): The dedication of the book to the Prophet Muhammad and his Companions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of the 'إهداء' (dedication) in this book?
A) To establish a legal ruling about the Prophet's miracles.
B) To invite students to memorize the Prophet's biography.
C) To express reverence for the Prophet Muhammad and his Companions before beginning the fiqh content.
D) To prove the authenticity of the Hadith about the lizard.

Key Takeaway
A devotional opening in a Hanafi fiqh text is not a source of law but a prefatory expression of reverence.

The Methodology of Imdad al-Fattah: Proofs without Dialectics
Editor introduces the book's sources and method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three primary sources cited in Imdad al-Fattah and explain why the author deliberately avoided dialectical argument (jadal).

Core Idea
Al-Shurunbulali's commentary relies solely on Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma, shunning debate to focus on Hanafi rulings.

The editor's preface to Imdad al-Fattah lays out the book's approach. Rather than engaging in inter-madhhab debates or lengthy dialectical arguments, the author presents direct proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus (ijma). This lesson will help you understand the methodological choice and its significance in Hanafi fiqh education.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and the base text
The editor introduces the book as a commentary (sharh) on Nur al-Idah, a matn on ibadat by al-Shurunbulali. Recognise that this is a foundational text for Hanafi worship rulings.
Nur al-Idah is widely studied in Hanafi circles.
2
Locate the primary sources mentioned
The passage explicitly states that proofs are derived from the Quran, the Sunnah, or Ijma. Note that Qiyas is not listed separately, as it is implied within the methodology of extracting rulings.
Ijma here refers to consensus of the scholars.
3
Understand the avoidance of jadal
The author says he intentionally kept the commentary free of dialectical argument (jadal) and rarely mentions opposing madhhabs. This is a deliberate pedagogical choice to focus on the Hanafi position with its own evidence.
Jadal in this context means argumentation that goes beyond simple proof, often involving hypothetical objections and responses.
4
Note the supporting evidence cited
The editor cites Quran 16:89 and a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about holding fast to the Book and Sunnah. These are used to justify the focus on primary sources.
The hadith is attributed to Malik's Muwatta'.
5
Appreciate the book's place in Hanafi literature
The editor calls it 'one of the mother books of Hanafi fiqh' (min ummahat kutub al-fiqh al-Hanafi). Recognise that this commentary is a key reference for students of the Hanafi school.
Ummahat kutub are the core, authoritative texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المحقق
الحمد الله الذي هدانا لدين خير الأنام، وعمنا بكبير الفضل والإحسان، فأرشدنا إلى تعلم أحكام كتابه العظيم وسنة نبيه الكريم، وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، القائل في كتابه وَنَزَّلْنَا عَلَيْكَ الْكِتَبَ تِبْيَنَا لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهُدًى وَرَحْمَةً وَبُشْرَى لِلْمُسْلِمِينَ ) الخ : ۸۹]، والصلاة والســـــلام على النبي الكريم القائل: تركت فيكم أمرين لن تضلوا ما تمسكتم بهما، كتاب الله وسنة نبيه » (۱) وعلى آله من الصحابة الكرام والأئمة المجتهدين، الذين استنبطوا لنا المسائل من مواردها واستخرجوها من كلام الله وسنة نبيه ومصطفاه.
وبعد: فإن كتاب إمداد الفتاح شرح نور الإيضاح ونجاة الأرواح لأبي الإخلاص حسـن بـن عمار الشرنبلالي المصري الحنفي، يُعد من أمهات كتب الفقه الحنفي، الذي عمل فيه مصنفه عـلـى شرح متنه المشهور « نور الإيضاح في العبادات، حيث انتهج في شرحه إيراد الأدلة لكل مسألة مستمدة من الكتاب العزيز، والسنة الشريفة، أو الإجماع، بعيداً عن طريقة الجدل في عرضه، أو إيراد أقوال أصحاب المذاهب الأخرى، قال في المقدمة مبيناً خطته (ص٢٥): وأخـلـيــت هـذا الشرح المبارك عن طريقة الجدل قصداً، وعن سياق مذهب المخالف إلا نزراً، وذكرت أدلة المسـائل مـن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع بقدر الحاجة والإمكان.
(1) أخرجه مالك في الموطأ (۸۹۹/٢).
Editor's Introduction. Praise be to Allah who guided us to the religion of the best of mankind, and overwhelmed us with great bounty and beneficence, so He guided us to learn the rulings of His Mighty Book and the Sunnah of His Noble Prophet. I bear witness that there is no god but Allah alone, with no partner, who says in His Book: 'And We have sent down to you the Book as clarification for all things and as guidance and mercy and good tidings for the Muslims.' (al-Nahl 89). And prayers and peace be upon the Noble Prophet who said: 'I have left among you two matters; you will never go astray as long as you hold fast to them: the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Prophet.' And upon his family from the noble Companions and the mujtahid imams, who deduced for us the issues from their sources and extracted them from the speech of Allah, the Sunnah of His Prophet, and the chosen one. After this: Indeed the book Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah wa Najat al-Arwah by Abi al-Ikhlas Hasan bin Ammar al-Shurunbulali al-Misri al-Hanafi is considered one of the mother books of Hanafi fiqh, in which its author worked on commenting on his well-known matn 'Nur al-Idah fi al-Ibadat', wherein he adopted in his commentary the presentation of proofs for every issue derived from the Mighty Book, the Noble Sunnah, or Ijma, far from the method of dialectic in its presentation or presenting the views of other madhhab followers. He said in the introduction clarifying his plan (p. 25): 'I have emptied this blessed commentary from the method of dialectic intentionally, and from the context of the opposing madhhab except for a little, and I have mentioned the proofs of the issues from the Book, the Sunnah, and Ijma to the extent of need and possibility.' (1) Recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta' (2/899).
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction (muqaddimat al-muhaqqiq) to the work Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah. The editor begins with the basmala, praising Allah for guidance to learn the rulings of the Quran and Sunnah. He cites Quran 16:89 and a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about holding fast to the Book and Sunnah. He then identifies the book and its author: Abu al-Ikhlas Hasan bin Ammar al-Shurunbulali al-Hanafi (d. 1069 AH), a prominent Hanafi jurist from Egypt. The commentary explains Nur al-Idah, a well-known matn on acts of worship (ibadat) in the Hanafi school. The editor highlights the author's methodology: he brings proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma (consensus) for each ruling, deliberately avoiding dialectical argument (jadal) and extensive reference to opposing schools. The author himself, in his original introduction, says he intentionally kept the commentary free of jadal and rarely mentions other madhhabs. The editor notes that the author also mentions the correct or preponderant view (al-mujzam bi-sihhatih) and sometimes the weak view as a warning. A footnote attributes the hadith to Malik's Muwatta.

Key Terms
الجدل (al-jadal): Dialectical argumentation; the author avoids it in his commentary.
الإجماع (al-ijma): Consensus of scholars as a source of law; one of the three primary sources mentioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what are the three primary sources the author uses for proofs?
A) Quran, Sunnah, Ijma
B) Quran, Sunnah, Qiyas
C) Quran, Sunnah, Urf
D) Quran, Ijma, Qiyas

Key Takeaway
Imdad al-Fattah is a Hanafi fiqh commentary that builds rulings on Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma, deliberately avoiding dialectical debate to present clear, direct proofs.

Methodology of Imdad al-Fattah: Evidence and Preference
Editorial introduction outlining the book's methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two key methodological features of Imdad al-Fattah and explain why they are important in Hanafi fiqh study.

Core Idea
Imdad al-Fattah grounds each ruling in evidence and clarifies which opinion is preferred.

The editor of Imdad al-Fattah opens with praise to Allah and the Prophet, then introduces the book's distinctive approach. This approach makes the commentary a powerful tool for learning Hanafi fiqh: it provides the evidence (dalil) for every ruling and clearly identifies the preferred (rajih) and outweighed (marjuh) opinions. Understanding this methodology will help you use the book effectively and deepen your grasp of fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main features
Read the editor's description: what two things does he say the book does differently? First, it mentions adillah (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma) for each issue. Second, it states both the rajih and marjuh opinions, with the reason for preference.
These are explicitly highlighted in the passage.
2
Understand the benefit of providing evidence
Why is it helpful to know the dalil for a ruling? The editor says it helps in understanding and memorizing the issue along with its source. It connects the ruling to its foundation.
The student should see the link between dalil and fiqh comprehension.
3
Appreciate the mention of rajih and marjuh
By presenting both the strong and weak opinions, the book gives a comprehensive view of the issue. The student develops a 'strong juristic faculty' (malakah fiqhiyyah) and knows which opinion to rely upon.
This is crucial for fatwa and practice.
4
Recognize the avoidance of debate
The author deliberately avoids detailed polemics (jadl) with other schools. This keeps the commentary focused and accessible, yet still rigorous.
This does not mean ignoring other views entirely; they are mentioned when needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم

مقدمة المحقق

الحمد لله الذي هدانا لدين خير الأنام، وعمنا بكبير الفضل والإحسان، فأرشدنا إلى تعلم أحكام كتابه العظيم وسنة نبيه الكريم، وأشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وحده لا شريك له، القائل في كتابه: ﴿وَنَزَّلْنَا عَلَيْكَ الْكِتَابَ تِبْيَانًا لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهُدًى وَرَحْمَةً وَبُشْرَى لِلْمُسْلِمِينَ﴾ [النحل: 89]، والصلاة والسلام على النبي الكريم القائل: «تركت فيكم أمرين لن تضلوا ما تمسكتم بهما: كتاب الله وسنة نبيه» (1) وعلى آله من الصحابة الكرام والأئمة المجتهدين، الذين استنبطوا لنا المسائل من مواردها واستخرجوها من كلام الله وسنة نبيه ومصطفاه.

وبعد: فإن كتاب «إمداد الفتاح شرح نور الإيضاح ونجاة الأرواح» لأبي الإخلاص حسن بن عمار الشرنبلالي المصري الحنفي، يُعد من أمهات كتب الفقه الحنفي، الذي عمل فيه مصنفه على شرح متنه المشهور «نور الإيضاح في العبادات»، حيث انتهج في شرحه إيراد الأدلة لكل مسألة مستمدة من الكتاب العزيز، والسنة الشريفة، أو الإجماع، بعيداً عن طريقة الجدل في عرضه، أو إيراد أقوال أصحاب المذاهب الأخرى. قال في المقدمة مبيناً خطته (ص 25): وأخلَّيت هذا الشرح المبارك عن طريقة الجدل قصداً، وعن سياق مذهب المخالف إلا نزراً، وذكرت أدلة المسائل من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع بقدر الحاجة والإمكان.

وكذلك يذكر ما جزم بصحته من المسائل، أو رجحه أهل التصحيح والترجيح، مع ذكر بيان وجه الترجيح، ويذكر أيضاً مقابل القول المرجح القول المرجوح، وإذا كان لجانبه قول غير صحيح ذكره منبهاً عليه.

وهاتان الميزتان هما من أهم ما يميز هذا الكتاب عن غيره من كتب المذهب الحنفي، فإنَّ ذكر دليل كلِّ مسألة يساعد على فهم وحفظ المسألة مع الأصل الذي استنبط منه، وإن ذكر الأقوال في المسألة الواحدة مع ذكر الراجح والمرجوح والصحيح منها والضعيف، يعطي طالب العلم إحاطة لأصل المسألة وملكة فقهية قوية.

وجمع أيضاً إلى هاتين الميزتين حسن التأليف، ووضوح العبارة، وحسن العرض، من غير إطالة في غير حاجة، ولا اختصار مضر في مقام الحاجة إليه، مع ما جمع فيه من الفروع المهمة والمسائل المفيدة.

هذا ومع نفاسته بقي هذا السفر بعيداً عن متناول طلبة العلم، إلى أن هيأ الله لنا شرف خدمته وإخراجه إلى إخواننا طلبة العلم، لتعم الفائدة كل طالب يطالع كتاب «المراقي» إذ هو أصله ومنه اختصر، سيما وقد لاقى قبولاً حسناً واهتماماً كبيراً من الطلبة والعلماء.

وأخيراً أرجو من الله تعالى لنا الثواب وحسن القبول، وأن ينفع به، وأن يجزي خيراً كل من أسهم في إخراجه وإعداده ونشره، وأخص بالشكر الشيخ زياد حمدان، وأسأل الله أن يجزيهم الجزاء الوافر، إنه تعالى سميع مجيب الدعاء. ﴿رَبَّنَا تَقَبَّلْ مِنَّا إِنَّكَ أَنْتَ السَّمِيعُ الْعَلِيمُ﴾ [البقرة: 127]، وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد وعلى آله وصحبه الكرام، والحمد لله رب العالمين.

دمشق في 12 / ربيع الأول / 1423 الموافق لـ: 23 / أيار / 2002

المحقق
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Editor's Introduction. Praise be to Allah, who guided us to the religion of the best of mankind, and bestowed upon us great favor and beneficence, so He guided us to learn the rulings of His Mighty Book and the Sunnah of His Noble Prophet. I bear witness that there is no god but Allah alone, without partner, who says in His Book: 'And We have sent down to you the Book as clarification for all things and as guidance and mercy and good tidings for the Muslims' (Surah al-Nahl: 89). Blessings and peace be upon the noble Prophet who said: 'I have left among you two matters, you will not go astray as long as you hold fast to them: the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Prophet.' And upon his family, the noble Companions, and the mujtahid Imams who extracted for us the rulings from their sources and deduced them from the speech of Allah and the Sunnah of His chosen Prophet. To proceed: The book 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah wa Najat al-Arwah' by Abu al-Ikhlas Hasan ibn Ammar al-Shurunbulali al-Misri al-Hanafi is considered one of the mother books of Hanafi fiqh, in which the author worked to explain his famous text 'Nur al-Idah fi al-'Ibadat'. He adopted in his commentary the method of presenting the evidences for each issue derived from the Mighty Book, the Noble Sunnah, or consensus, far from the method of debate in its presentation or mentioning the views of the other schools. He said in the introduction outlining his plan (p. 25): 'I have deliberately kept this blessed commentary free from the method of debate, and from detailing the opposing school except sparingly. And I have mentioned the evidences for the issues from the Book, Sunnah, and consensus to the extent of need and possibility.' Likewise, he mentions what has been definitively deemed correct from the issues, or what the scholars of verification and preference have preferred, along with explaining the reason for preference. He also mentions the preferred opinion alongside the outweighed opinion, and if there is an incorrect opinion alongside it, he mentions it while warning about it. These two characteristics are among the most important features distinguishing this book from others of the Hanafi school. Mentioning the evidence for each issue helps in understanding and memorizing the issue along with the principle from which it was derived. And mentioning the opinions in a single issue along with the preferred, outweighed, sound, and weak ones gives the student a comprehensive understanding of the origin of the issue and a strong juristic faculty. In addition to these two characteristics, the book combines good composition, clarity of expression, and good presentation, without unnecessary length or harmful brevity in places where it is needed, along with the important branches and beneficial issues it contains. Despite its value, this work remained out of reach for students until Allah prepared for us the honor of serving it and bringing it out to our brethren students, so that benefit may spread to every student who studies the book 'al-Maraqi', since it is its source and from which it was summarized, especially since it met with good acceptance and great interest from students and scholars. Finally, I ask Allah Almighty for reward and good acceptance, and that He benefits through it, and reward everyone who contributed to its production, preparation, and publication. I especially thank Shaykh Ziyad Hamdan, and I ask Allah to give them abundant reward. Indeed, He is All-Hearing, Responsive to prayer. (Our Lord, accept from us. Indeed You are the All-Hearing, the All-Knowing) [al-Baqarah: 127]. May Allah bless our master Muhammad and his family and noble Companions, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds. Damascus, 12 Rabi' al-Awwal 1423 AH, corresponding to 23 May 2002. The Editor.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This is the editor's introduction (muqaddimah al-muhaqqiq) to the book 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah'. The editor begins with the basmalah, hamdalah, and shahadah, praising Allah for guidance and thanking Him for enabling the learning of Islamic rulings. He cites Quran 16:89 to highlight the comprehensiveness of the Quran, and a hadith about holding fast to the Book and Sunnah. This establishes the reliance on primary sources. He then introduces the author, Abu al-Ikhlas al-Shurunbulali, a Hanafi scholar, and the book itself, which is a commentary on the famous matn 'Nur al-Idah fi al-'Ibadat'. The editor highlights two key features of the commentary: (1) it provides evidence (adillah) from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma for each ruling, avoiding unnecessary polemics, and (2) it mentions the preferred opinion (al-rajih) alongside the outweighed one (al-marjuh), noting the reason for preference. This approach helps the student understand the derivation of rulings and develop strong fiqh intuition. The editor also praises the book's style, clarity, and balance between brevity and expansion. He explains that the book had been inaccessible but now, through this edition, it is available, especially benefiting those studying 'al-Maraqi' (likely referring to 'Maraqi al-Sa'adat'). He thanks contributors and prays for acceptance. The editor signs off with date and place. This introduction does not contain any legal rulings but sets the methodological framework for the commentary.

Key Terms
نور الإيضاح (Nur al-Idah): The famous Hanafi matn on 'ibadat authored by al-Shurunbulali, which the commentary Imdad al-Fattah explains.
إمداد الفتاح (Imdad al-Fattah): The title of the commentary being introduced: 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah wa Najat al-Arwah'.
الراجح والمرجوح (al-rajih wa al-marjuh): The preferred opinion and the outweighed opinion in a given legal issue, with explanation of the reason for preference.
طريقة الجدل (tariq al-jadal): The method of debate or polemics, which the author deliberately avoids to keep the commentary focused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two main features does the editor highlight about 'Imdad al-Fattah'?
A) It includes proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma, and it mentions both the rajih and marjuh opinions.
B) It is a brief summary of Hanafi fiqh with no references.
C) It only discusses worship (ibadat) and ignores other topics.
D) It includes debates with other schools in detail.

Key Takeaway
Imdad al-Fattah's methodology of combining evidence with clear preference designation makes it an exceptional tool for building a solid foundation in Hanafi fiqh.

Why a Hanafi Fiqh Book Includes Evidence and Multiple Opinions
Editor describes book's unique features and methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain two key features of a well-structured Hanafi fiqh commentary: providing dalil for each ruling and distinguishing between rajih and marjuh opinions.

Core Idea
A Hanafi fiqh book that cites evidence and ranks opinions builds both memory and legal judgment.

You are about to study a classical Hanafi fiqh text. Before diving into rulings, it is vital to understand how the book itself is structured. The editor's preface reveals two pillars of sound fiqh learning: knowing the proof behind each ruling, and recognizing which opinion is stronger. These features are not mere decoration—they are essential tools for developing juristic insight (malakah fiqhiyyah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main features
Read the editor's claim: the book is distinguished by (1) mentioning the dalil for every issue, and (2) stating multiple opinions with clear rajih/marjuh classification. Write down both features in your own words.
These are directly stated in the first sentence of the Arabic text.
2
Understand the pedagogical benefit
The editor says these features help with 'fahm wa hifz' (understanding and memorizing) the ruling with its principle, and they give 'ihatah' (comprehensive knowledge) of the issue and a 'malakah fiqhiyyah qawiyyah' (strong juristic faculty). Explain in one sentence how each feature contributes to this.
3
Locate the additional merits
Beyond the two main features, the editor lists good composition, clear expression, and balanced length. Also note the inclusion of important branches (furu') and beneficial issues. List three of these additional merits.
These are found later in the first paragraph of the Arabic text.
4
Apply to your own study method
When you study a Hanafi text, always look for the dalil and ask: 'What is the evidence? Is this the strong opinion or a weak one?' Practice this by recalling a ruling you already know (e.g., wudu is broken by sleep) and state its dalil (e.g., hadith: 'The eye is the drawstring of the posterior') and the madhhab's position (rajih).
This step moves from theory to personal application.
5
Preview the editorial work
The editor lists ten tasks (e.g., collating manuscripts, correcting text, adding indices). Recognize that a verified edition (tahqiq) requires this rigor. As a student, you benefit from a reliable text with proper citations. Skim the list to appreciate the scholarly effort behind the book you are reading.

My work in verifying this book: 1- Copying the first manuscript (marked B) and collating it, then comparing it with the other manuscript copy. 2- Attributing Quranic verses. 3- Extracting prophetic hadiths and reports from the collections of the Prophetic Sunnah. 4- I translated the notable figures mentioned in this book, as well as the books, and placed them in a separate register at the end of the book. 5- I documented the texts of the book by attributing them to their sources in our hands. 6- I explained rare words. 7- I commented on some phrases to resolve their difficulties and clarify their ambiguities, benefiting from the marginalia of Shaykh Shakir on 'Al-Maraqi' or 'Hashiyat al-Tahtawi', and I denoted the former with (Sh) and the latter with (T). 8- I placed the text of Nur al-Idah at the top of the pages and distinguished it in red in the commentary. 9- I placed titles for the chapters according to the book 'Maraqi al-Falah' and 'Hashiyat al-Tahtawi'. 10- I equipped the book with multiple indices for verses, hadiths, and topics. This, and we ask Allah the Most High, the Almighty, to forgive any oversight or shortcoming from us, hoping for His acceptance. And may Allah bless our master Muhammad and his family and noble companions.

Commentary
This passage is the editor's preface to the verification of the book 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah'. The editor opens by highlighting two key features of this commentary: first, it provides the evidence (dalil) for each ruling, which aids understanding and memorization by linking the ruling to its original source of derivation. Second, it presents multiple opinions on a single issue, specifying which is preponderant (rajih), weaker (marjuh), correct (sahih), or weak (da'if). This gives the student comprehensive knowledge and develops a strong juristic faculty. The editor then notes additional merits: good composition, clear expression, and balanced presentation—neither overly lengthy nor harmfully brief. Despite its value, the book was not widely accessible until the editor undertook its publication. He expresses hope for reward and thanks Shaykh Ziyad Hamdan. The second part enumerates ten steps of the editor's scholarly work: copying and collating manuscripts, citing Quranic verses, extracting hadiths, providing biographical notes on figures, documenting sources, explaining rare words, adding annotations from two commentaries (Shakir and Tahtawi), placing the matn (Nur al-Idah) at the top of pages in red, titling chapters according to 'Maraqi al-Falah' and 'Hashiyat al-Tahtawi', and compiling indices. The preface concludes with a supplication for forgiveness and acceptance.

Key Terms
المذهب الحنفي (al-madhhab al-hanafi): The Hanafi school of Islamic law, founded by Imam Abu Hanifa, which the book follows.
دليل (dalil): Evidence from Quran, Sunnah, or other sources that supports a legal ruling.
راجح (rajih): The preponderant or stronger opinion among multiple views.
مرجوح (marjuh): The weaker opinion, surpassed by the rajih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The two features that make a Hanafi fiqh commentary pedagogically powerful are citing the dalil for every ruling and clearly distinguishing between rajih and marjuh opinions.

Understanding the Editorial Methodology of Imdad al-Fattah
Lists the ten editorial steps followed by the editor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ten editorial steps used in critical editions of Hanafi texts and explain their purpose.

Core Idea
A critical edition (tahqiq) is the backbone of reliable fiqh study — it verifies the text, sources, and commentary.

This passage from the editor of Imdad al-Fattah outlines a meticulous method for producing a reliable edition of a classical Hanafi text. For a student of fiqh, understanding this method is crucial: it shows how we can trust the text we are reading. It also introduces key concepts like matn (core text) and hashya (gloss commentary).

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's list of steps
Read through the ten steps carefully. Note the numerical order and the specific actions described in each step.
This list is the core of the lesson.
2
Group the steps by function
Categorize: Steps 1-3 deal with manuscript comparison and sourcing; Steps 4-6 involve annotation and documentation; Steps 7-8 focus on layout and formatting; Steps 9-10 are for indexing.
This helps understand the different dimensions of tahqiq.
3
Examine the footnote symbols
In step 7, the editor mentions two glosses (Shakir and al-Tahtawi) symbolized by (ش) and (ط). Recognize that these symbols allow the reader to identify the source of marginal comments.
Such symbols are common in critical editions.
4
Assess the completeness
The editor concludes with a prayer for forgiveness and acceptance. This reflects the adab (etiquette) of scholarly work — humility before Allah.
Observe that the editorial work is framed as a service, not mere technical exercise.

My work in verifying this book: 1- Transcribing the first manuscript marked (B) and collating it, then comparing it with the other manuscript. 2- Attributing Qur'anic verses. 3- Extracting Prophetic hadiths and reports from the collections of the Prophetic Sunnah. 4- Translating the proper names mentioned in this book, as well as the books, and placing them in a separate appendix at the end of the book. 5- I documented the texts of the book by attributing them to their sources that are in our hands. 6- I explained strange expressions. 7- I commented on some phrases, solving their difficulties and clarifying their obscurity, benefiting from the gloss of Shaykh Shakir on al-Maraqi or the gloss of al-Tahtawi, and I symbolized the first with (Sh) and the second with (T). 8- I placed the text of Nur al-Idah at the top of the pages and distinguished it in red in the commentary. 9- I placed chapter headings according to the book Maraqi al-Falah and the gloss of al-Tahtawi. 10- I provided the book with multiple indexes for verses, hadiths, and topics. Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is the editor's description of his methodology. It lists ten steps: (1) transcribing the first manuscript marked (ب) and comparing it with the other manuscript; (2) attributing Qur'anic verses to their chapters; (3) extracting hadiths and reports from standard collections; (4) translating proper names and book titles and placing them in a separate index; (5) verifying text citations by referencing available sources; (6) explaining difficult words; (7) adding marginal comments based on two glosses (Shakir's on Maraqi and al-Tahtawi's), symbolized (ش) and (ط); (8) placing the matn of Nur al-Idah at the top of pages in red ink; (9) organizing chapter headings according to Maraqi al-Falah and al-Tahtawi's gloss; (10) providing comprehensive indexes. The editor ends with a prayer for forgiveness and acceptance. This front matter is typical of a critical edition, showing the editor's careful approach to preserving the text.

Key Terms
تحقيق (tahqiq): Critical edition: scholarly verification and annotation of a manuscript.
متن (matn): The original text of Nur al-Idah, placed at the top of pages in red.
حاشية (hashiya): Gloss or marginal commentary, here specifically by Shaykh Shakir and al-Tahtawi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The editorial methodology ensures textual reliability, source citation, and reader accessibility, forming the foundation for serious engagement with Hanafi fiqh.

Understanding Manuscript Sources in Hanafi Texts
Describes two manuscripts used for the critical edition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify manuscript symbols and explain their role in verifying a Hanafi text's authenticity.

Core Idea
Manuscript symbols (رموز) are keys to tracing a text's transmission chain (isnad).

Critical editions of classical Hanafi works rely on handwritten manuscripts. Scholars assign symbols to each copy to track their provenance and quality. This lesson introduces you to two manuscripts used for the edition of Nur al-Idah, a foundational text in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding how editors evaluate manuscripts helps you appreciate the care taken to preserve the school's rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the manuscripts
Read the passage and note the two manuscripts: one from al-Zahiriyya (symbol ب) and a private copy (symbol م).
Both are handwritten copies (نسخ خطية).
2
Examine their features
Compare the number of folios, lines per page, and coverage. The first ends at the book of Hajj; the second ends at the book of I'tikaf. Observe that the matn is distinguished in red in the first.
These details indicate completeness and quality.
3
Recognize the symbols
The editor uses (ب) for the Zahiriyya copy and (م) for the private copy. Note that symbols are a shorthand used in critical apparatus.
Knowing symbols helps you read footnotes in later lessons.
4
Appreciate the role of editors
Editors rely on such manuscripts to establish a reliable text. The private copy has errors (تصحيف وتحريف), so the editor cross-checks with the better copy.
This is part of the naqd al-nusus (textual criticism) methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصف الأصول المعتمدة
تم الاعتماد في تحقيق الكتاب على نسختين خطيتين:
الأولى: النسخة الظاهرية، وهي تحت رقم (٩٦٤٨) في مكتب الأسد الوطنية بدمشق، وهي نسخة جيدة، منقولة عن نسخة المؤلف وقد انتهت بآخر كتاب الحج، وعدد أوراقها (٣٨٤) ورقة، في كل ورقة (۲۳) سطراً، مُيّز فيها المتن بالحمرة، وقد رمزت لها بـ (ب).
الثانية: نسخة خاصة، أتحفنا بها أحد الأفاضل الكرام من مكتبته الخاصة، جزاه الله عنا خير

Description of the approved sources. The verification of the book relied on two manuscripts: The first: the al-Zahiriyya manuscript, which is under number (9648) in the Assad National Library in Damascus. It is a good copy, transmitted from the author's copy, and it ends with the last chapter of Hajj. Its number of folios is (384), each folio has (23) lines. The matn is distinguished in red, and I symbolized it as (ب). The second: a private copy, gifted to us by one of the esteemed gentlemen from his private library, may Allah reward him richly. It is a readable copy, containing some misspellings and distortions. It ends at the end of the chapter of I'tikaf. Its number of folios is (302), each folio has (25) lines, and I symbolized it as (م). 10-

Commentary
This passage describes the two manuscripts used in the critical edition. The first manuscript, symbolized (ب), is from the al-Zahiriyya collection (now in the Assad National Library in Damascus, number 9648). It is a good copy directly from the author's copy, covering up to the end of the Book of Hajj, with 384 folios, 23 lines per folio, with the matn highlighted in red. The second manuscript, symbolized (م), is a private copy donated by a generous individual. It is readable but contains some errors and distortions, and ends at the end of the Book of I'tikaf, with 302 folios, 25 lines per folio. The editor used these two to prepare the present edition.

Key Terms
نسخة خطية (nuskha khattiyya): Handwritten manuscript used as a source for the edition.
متن (matn): The base text of Nur al-Idah, highlighted in red in the first manuscript.
رمز (ramz): Symbol assigned to each manuscript: (ب) for al-Zahiriyya and (م) for the private copy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) (م)
B) (ب)
C) (ش)
D) (ط)

Key Takeaway
Manuscript symbols are essential tools in Hanafi textual criticism, allowing scholars to identify sources and assess their reliability.

Editor's Preface and Manuscript Sources
Editor's preface and author's hamd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two manuscript copies used in this edition and state the significance of each source for textual reliability.

Core Idea
Manuscript provenance is foundational—trustworthy copies ensure accurate transmission of fiqh rulings.

Before diving into fiqh rulings, it is essential to understand the textual foundation. This lesson introduces the two manuscripts used to edit 'Imdad al-Fattah'. Knowing their origins and characteristics allows you to assess the reliability of the text you are studying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the manuscripts
Locate the two manuscripts described: the Zahiriyyah copy (B) and the private copy (M). Note their shelfmarks, folio counts, and line counts.
Manuscript B ends with Kitab al-Hajj; Manuscript M ends with Bab al-I’tikaf.
2
Compare their quality
The editor states that (B) is 'good' and clearly highlights the matn in red, while (M) is readable but has misprints and distortions.
This quality difference affects the editor's choice of primary source.
3
Understand the editor's methodology
The editor uses symbols (B) and (M) for concise reference. This practice is standard in critical editions to maintain clarity while documenting variants.
You will see these symbols in footnotes.
4
Recognize the author's hamd
After the editor's preface, the author's text begins with the basmalah and praise of Allah. This is a traditional opening, though the OCR is garbled.
Even corrupted, the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' is recognizable.

تم الاعتماد في تحقيق الكتاب على نسختين خطيتين:
الأولى: النسخة الظاهرية، وهي تحت رقم (٩٦٤٨) في مكتب الأسد الوطنية بدمشق، وهي نسخة جيدة، منقولة عن نسخة المؤلف وقد انتهت بآخر كتاب الحج، وعدد أوراقها (٣٨٤) ورقة، في كل ورقة (۲۳) سطراً، مُيّز فيها المتن بالحمرة، وقد رمزت لها بـ (ب).

الصفحة الأخيرة من المخطوط (ب)
Description of the relied-upon sources. The editing of the book relied on two manuscripts: First: the Zahiriyyah manuscript, number (9648) in the National Library of Asad in Damascus. It is a good copy, transcribed from the author's copy, ending with the last chapter of Hajj. It has 384 folios, each folio 23 lines, with the matn highlighted in red. I have designated it as (B). Second: a private manuscript, gifted to us by a gracious person from his private library—may Allah reward him well. It is a readable copy with some misprints and distortions, ending with the last chapter of I'tikaf. It has 302 folios, each folio 25 lines. I have designated it as (M). In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah who created everything by His power... [remainder is garbled and cannot be reliably translated]
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is from the front matter of the book 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah'. It contains two parts: first, the editor's description of the two manuscript copies used for the critical edition; second, the beginning of the author's own text, which starts with the basmalah and hamd (praise of Allah). The editor identifies the first manuscript as the Zahiriyyah copy (B) from Damascus, noting its good quality and red highlighting of the matn. The second manuscript (M) is a private copy with some textual errors, ending at a different point. The author then opens his work with an ornate Arabic praise of Allah, though the OCR is badly garbled. This type of front matter is common in scholarly editions to establish textual authority and document sources.

Key Terms
الأصول المعتمدة (al-usul al-mu'tamadah): The relied-upon manuscripts used as sources for the critical edition.
النسخة الظاهرية (al-nuskhah al-Zahiriyyah): The manuscript from the Zahiriyyah library (now part of the National Library of Damascus).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
The Introduction of a Hanafi Fiqh Commentary
Author's opening praise and prayer, introducing the commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a Hanafi fiqh commentary's introduction and explain the function of 'al-salatu 'imadu al-din' as a memorization anchor.

Core Idea
The introduction frames the work's purpose and authority; every phrase is intentionally placed to honour Allah, the Prophet, and the science of fiqh.

This passage is the opening of 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah', a Hanafi fiqh commentary. The author begins with the basmala, praise to Allah, and salutations upon the Prophet, then highlights the virtue of prayer as the pillar of religion. Such introductions are not mere formalities—they establish the work's spiritual and scholarly foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basmala and hamd
Locate the author's opening words. The basmala ('In the name of Allah...') and hamd ('Praise be to Allah') are standard. Note the specific attributes mentioned (e.g., 'who created everything by His power').
Even with poor OCR, you can recognize the basmala by its common pattern.
2
Find the salawat
Search for salutations upon the Prophet Muhammad. This often follows the hamd and uses phrases like 'al-salatu wa al-salam 'alayhi'. It signals respect and seeking blessings.
3
Extract the key phrase
The text includes 'al-salatu 'imadu al-din' (Prayer is the pillar of religion). This is a memorable anchor used to emphasize the centrality of salah. Note its placement and rhetorical force.
This phrase is often used in fiqh texts to motivate learning prayer rulings.
4
Analyse the author's purpose
The author states he relies on divine aid to complete the explanation. This shows humility and acknowledges that knowledge comes from Allah. It also signals that the commentary aims to clarify fiqh rulings.
This is typical of Hanafi scholars: they begin by invoking Allah's help.
5
Summarise the function of each component
Each part of the introduction serves a distinct purpose: basmala for blessing, hamd for gratitude, salawat for honoring the Prophet, and the key phrase to highlight the importance of the subject.
Understanding this structure helps you navigate any classical fiqh text.

Source Text

In the name of the Guardian, the Merciful. Praise be to Allah who created everything by His power and made His signs manifest by His management... He is the one who supports the religion and establishes the believers... I seek His help to explain this introduction... Prayer is the pillar of religion and the firm handhold... This is the commentary on the book by the grace of Allah, the Generous, the Bestower...

Commentary
This passage is the opening front matter of the commentary 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah'. The author begins with the basmala, praise to Allah, and salutations upon the Prophet. He describes the virtue of knowledge and the importance of understanding the rulings of fiqh. The text is highly ornamental and includes many rhetorical flourishes typical of classical Islamic scholarly introductions. The author mentions his reliance on divine aid to complete the explanation. Due to the poor OCR quality, the exact wording is uncertain, but the overall structure is standard for a fiqh commentary's preamble.

Key Terms
الصلاة عماد الدين (al-salatu ‘imadu al-din): Prayer is the pillar/support of the religion, meaning it is essential and foundational.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The introduction of a Hanafi fiqh commentary is an essential component that establishes the work's spiritual foundation, methodology, and motivational framework.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Opening Praise and the Centrality of Salah
Opening praise and introduction highlighting salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose of the basmalah and hamdalah in a classical fiqh text and state why salah is described as the pillar of religion in the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Salah is the pillar of religion – the most important act of worship after iman.

Classical fiqh texts often begin with praise of Allah and salutations upon the Prophet. This opening passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, not only praises Allah and honors the Prophet but also immediately establishes the central importance of salah. Understanding this framework helps the student appreciate the weight the Hanafi school places on prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the elements of the opening
Locate the basmalah, hamdalah, praise of creation, honor of the Prophet, and the statement about salah.
The basmalah is corrupted in the text; understand the intended phrase.
2
Explain the purpose of each element
Describe why a fiqh work typically starts with praise and blessings, and how it sets a spiritual tone.
3
Analyze the phrase 'الصلاة عماد الدين'
Break down the phrase: 'salah is the pillar of religion'. Why is it a pillar? What does that imply for its obligation?
This phrase is often cited as a hadith-like expression.
4
Connect to Hanafi fiqh
State the Hanafi ruling: salah is fard 'ayn, and the most important obligation after iman. This phrase reinforces its priority.

Source Text

الحَمْدُ لله. (۲) بسم الله الحر الحمر ( الحمد الله ) الذي خلق كل شيء بقدرته، وأوجده في آن ظهوره بإرادته، وأحكم أمره، فلا راد له بقوته، وأيَّد الدين، وأيد الذين جعلهم لحفظه خلائف، ورفع بعضهم فوق بعض درجات ليبلوهم، فيُظهر المطيع الخائف والآمن المخالف، وأسعد من حمل الأمانة بتوفيقه؛ لحفظها وقيامه بواجب حقها، وقد أبت السموات والأرض والجبال عن حملها، وأرشده لمعرفة أحكامها، وأخذها عن أهلها بغاية إحكامها، لما أنها المقصودة بالذات، وحقه على خلقه وسره المستودع في أشرف ذات، وأعظم القربات، فتسنم قنن المعالي، وتدرج فنن العوالي، وأحاط بأرجائه الحصن الحصين، وتمتعت حواسه بالحسناء المحلاة بجميع محاسن الدين، واختصه بحضرة التقريب، وناجاه القريب المجيب بسره، فَسُرَّ فؤاده وطرد عن جسمه الرقيب، ألا الصلاة عماد الدين، والعروة الوثقى والمتمسك المتين. ولما أن كانت المقدمة التي أراد الله سبحانه إيجادها، ومن بفيضه وفضله على الإخوان باستفادة أحكامها وإمدادها، مشتملة على أحكامها ، قد حلت برتبة تجل عن مقام الثريا ، وجلت محاسنها عن مماثلة جمال زينب سوريا.... وهي
Praise be to Allah. (2) In the name of Allah, the Compassionate, the Merciful? (Praise be to Allah) Who created everything with His power, and brought it into existence at the moment of its appearance by His will, and made His command firm, so there is no repeller to His power, and supported the religion, and supported those whom He made successors for its preservation, and raised some of them above others in degrees to test them, so that the obedient fearful and the secure disobedient may appear, and the most fortunate of those who bore the trust with His success, for its preservation and fulfillment of its due obligation, while the heavens, earth, and mountains refused to carry it, and guided him to know its rulings, and took it from its people with utmost precision, because it is the intended goal, and His right upon His creation, and His secret stored in the most noble essence, and the greatest acts of worship. So he ascended the peaks of excellences, and progressed through the branches of high stations, and encompassed the fortified fortress, and his senses enjoyed the beautiful adorned with all the beauties of religion, and He distinguished him with the presence of proximity, and the near Responding One whispered to him with His secret, so his heart was delighted and the watcher was expelled from his body. Indeed, salah is the pillar of religion, the firmest handhold, and the strong grip. And when the introduction that Allah—glorified be He—willed to bring into existence, and from His overflow and favor upon the brothers through deriving its rulings and its support, containing its rulings, has attained a rank that transcends the station of the Pleiades, and its beauties have become sublime from resembling the beauty of Zaynab of Syria... and it is
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the commentary, beginning with the basmalah and hamdalah. The author praises Allah for His creation, power, and support of the religion, then highlights the honor of the Prophet Muhammad (upon whom be peace) as the carrier of the trust. He then emphasizes that salah (prayer) is the pillar of religion, the firmest handhold. This sets the stage for the subject of the book, which is a commentary on Nur al-Idah, a text on fiqh, particularly taharah and salah. The author then introduces the 'muqaddimah' (introduction) that he intends to write, which is the commentary itself, praising its high rank and beauty. The text abruptly ends with 'wa hiya' (and it is), leading into the next part.

Key Terms
الصلاة عماد الدين (al-salah 'imad al-din): Salah is the pillar of religion; a well-known hadith-like phrase emphasizing its central importance.

Key Takeaway
The opening praise establishes that salah is the pillar of religion, emphasizing its central role in Islamic practice and Hanafi fiqh.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Author's Introduction and the Importance of Basmalah
Author's preface with praise of Allah and salah, metaphor for the text, and citation of basmalah hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose of a Hanafi fiqh introduction and identify the hadith on beginning matters with Bismillah.

Core Idea
The basmalah and praise of prayer are essential; the author humbly accepts writing a sharh.

The author begins with praise of Allah and praise of prayer (salah) as the pillar of religion. He then uses a poetic metaphor describing the original text as a veiled virgin that he was commanded to explain. This sets the stage for his commentary, reminding us that every important act should begin with bismillah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's opening
Read the first lines: 'الحمد لله' and praise of Allah's power. Note the emphasis on salah as 'عماد الدين'.
This is a standard Islamic introduction structure.
2
Analyze the metaphor
The author likens the text (Nur al-Idah) to a veiled virgin who has reached maturity. Understand that this is a rhetorical device to elevate the text's status.
The metaphor continues: suitors (scholars) were unable to explain it until the author was chosen.
3
Recognize the author's motivation
He explains that he was commanded by 'some of the knowers of Allah' (al-'arifin billah) to write this explanation.
This reflects the humility and chain of transmission in Islamic scholarship.
4
Locate the hadith on basmalah
At the end, the author quotes the hadith: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with Bismillah is ajdham (cut off/incomplete).'
This hadith is used to justify starting the main content with basmalah.
5
Note the title and prayer for acceptance
The author names his work 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah wa Najat al-Arwah' and prays for its benefit and acceptance.
This shows the author's hope that his work will be blessed.

Praise be to Allah. In the name of Allah, the Living, the Merciful. (Praise be to Allah) who created everything by His power, and brought it into existence at the moment of its appearance by His will, and perfected His command, so there is no repeller for it by His strength; and He supported the religion, and strengthened those whom He made as successors for its preservation, and raised some of them above others in degrees to test them, so that He may show the obedient fearful and the secure disobedient; and the most fortunate is the one who carried the trust with His success: for its preservation and fulfilling the duty of its right, while the heavens, earth, and mountains refused to carry it; and He guided him to knowledge of its rulings, and took it from its people with utmost perfection, because it is the intended object by essence, and His right upon His creation, and His secret deposited in the noblest essence, and the greatest acts of worship. So he ascended the peaks of highness, and traversed the branches of loftiness, and encompassed its corners with the strong fortress; and his senses enjoyed the beautiful one adorned with all the beauties of the religion; and He distinguished him with the station of nearness, and the Near One, the Answerer, conversed with him secretly, so his heart rejoiced and the watcher was expelled from his body. Indeed, prayer is the pillar of religion, the firm handhold and the strong grip. And when the preface that Allah wanted to bring into existence, and from His abundance and grace upon the brethren through deriving its rulings and its assistance, included its rulings, it had attained a rank that transcends the station of Pleiades, and its beauties excelled beyond resembling the beauty of Zainab of Syria... And she is veiled in her chamber, a virgin, until she reached the age of puberty, twelve years and a month; no suitor could match her, and no seeker approached her chamber; for she is a light untouched by fire; the flash of its lightning almost takes away the eyesight; and her dowry is a pearl in an ocean, a diver rarely reaches its bottom; and as if her dawn had appeared, and her muezzin called out: 'Come to success'; the full moon longed to propose to her, and the solution longed to be in her station; so she appeared to him, emerging from her strong veil, revealing her wonderful beauty by throwing off her niqab, inclining towards him with her sides, responding with eloquent speech saying: 'O, is there a witness upon him from the tongue of his proposal, so that it becomes permissible for him to propose to his own marriage-flesh?' The tongue of the situation said: 'Yes, for the matter depends on the belief of the judge, not on you; and the mother was neither a possessor of marriage-flesh nor of self; you are merely a foundling, nothing more, so no loss; and the mother also was purified from impurities and had withdrawn from men.' So the cause of separation and parting had become clear, and the cause of connection by virtue had become clear. So he showed gratitude with increase for the abundance of seeking assistance from the word of Tawhid by stripping it: 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah,' perpetually without end, because some of the knowers of Allah (may Allah return to us from their blessings and their support in this world on the day of meeting Allah) commanded me to explain that preface. So I complied with the noble command, and relied on my weak state upon the generosity of the All-Knowing, the Kind; and I sought aid from His abundant overflow; and I entrusted my affair to Him; He is sufficient for me and the best of Trustees. So the explanation of the book was completed by the favor of Allah, the Generous, the Giver; and I ask Him from His bounty, seeking intercession through our master Muhammad, the Chosen, the Elect, and the honored ones with Him, that He benefit all students thereby until the Day of Return, and that He make it purely for His noble Face, and that He protect it from the evil of every envious and sinful slanderer. And I have cast it into the sea of entrustment openly, so that the Generous One may accept it with good acceptance and cause it to grow beautifully, for the continuity of His Sharia and the revival of the Sunnah of His beloved and chosen one. May Allah send blessings and peace upon him, and upon all his fathers and his brethren among the prophets and the angels brought near, and upon his family, companions, and progeny, and the followers until the Day of Judgment. And I named it: 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah wa Najat al-Arwah'. And I began the book, saying: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful,' following the Book of Allah, and acting upon the saying of our master Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim is cut off (ajdham).' And in another narration:
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to his commentary 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah'. He begins with praise of Allah (hamd), extolling His power and wisdom in creation, and then specifically praises prayer (salah) as the pillar of religion (al-salah 'imad al-din). This is a common theme in Islamic texts. The author then uses an extended metaphor comparing the original text (Nur al-Idah) to a veiled virgin who has reached maturity, sought by no suitor until the author himself is commanded by a saintly figure (some of the 'arifin billah) to explain it. He humbly accepts and compiles this commentary, naming it 'Imdad al-Fattah Sharh Nur al-Idah wa Najat al-Arwah'. He concludes the introduction by opening his book with the basmalah, citing the well-known hadith that every important matter not beginning with bismillah is cut off (ajdham). This sets the tone for the work as a Hanafi fiqh commentary.

Key Terms
الصلاة (al-salah): The canonical prayer in Islam, described here as the pillar of religion (عماد الدين).
المقدمة (al-muqaddimah): The preface or introduction to the original matn (Nur al-Idah) which the author is commenting on.

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
The Basmalah: The Meaning of 'Ism' (Name)
Linguistic and theological analysis of the basmalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the grammatical structure of 'Bismillah' and state the Hanafi position that the name (ism) and the named (musamma) are identical.

Core Idea
In the basmalah, 'ism' means either the utterance of the name or is redundant, and the name and named are one according to Ahl al-Sunnah.

The basmalah is a phrase we recite daily, but its precise meaning has been debated. This lesson unpacks the grammatical and theological issues surrounding the word 'ism' in 'Bismillah'. It addresses how the name relates to the named and why the construction requires careful analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical issue with the ba'
Examine the function of the letter 'ba' in 'Bismillah'. Is it for transitivity (ta'diyah) or something else? The source suggests that treating it as transitive makes the meaning 'I have presented His name upon the intended object.'
This affects the overall meaning of the sentence.
2
Understand the problem of adding 'ism' to Allah
Since the name and named are considered one, adding the name to Allah seems redundant. The passage addresses this by questioning how 'ism' can be added to Allah if Allah is the name.
This leads to various interpretations.
3
Review the two proposed solutions
First solution: 'ism' here means 'tasmia' (the utterance of the name), so the estimation is 'By the remembrance of Allah I begin.' Second solution: 'ism' is redundant, similar to the phrase 'calling him by the name of water' meaning calling him by water.
The redundant interpretation repels the suspicion of an oath.
4
Note the Ahl al-Sunnah view on ism and musamma
The source states that according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, the name and the named are one thing. This is a theological affirmation that the name of Allah is not separate from His essence.
This is a key Hanafi position.
5
Conclude the meaning of the basmalah
Based on the analysis, the basmalah is understood as beginning with the name of Allah, either by uttering it or by starting with His name in a more direct sense.
The basmalah is a blessed invocation.

المخلوقين (۱) وهم أتباع أبي الحسن الأشعري، وأبي منصور الماتريدي ، قال : « لا تزال طائفة من أمتي ظـاهـريـن عـلـى الحق لا يضرهم من خالفهم حتى يأتي أمر الله وهم على ذلك .ط. والحديث أخرجه مسلم في الإمارة، (1) دون بعض، وخصوص الرحيم باعتبار اختصاصه ببعض المخلوقين، وهم المؤمنين خاصة. والرحمة: رقة القلب، وهي كيفية نفسانية تستحيل في حقه تعالى، فهي إما مجاز مرسل في الإحسان، فتكون صفة فعل، أو في إرادته، فتكون صفة ذات، وإما تمثيل : بأن مثل فعله تعالى بحال مَلِك عطف على رعيته، ورق لهم، فعمهم بمعروفه، فأطلق عليه الاسم، وأُريد به غايته التي هي : فعل، أو إرادة، لا بدوه الذي هو انفعال، فهو استعارة تمثيلية وبنيت الصفة المشبهة ) من رحم مع أنه متعد بجعله لازماً، أو نقله إلى فعل بالضم، وهذا كله مبني على أن الرحمن صفة، وهو كذلك في الأصل. لكنه صار علماً بالغلبة. فقد قال ابن هشام : الحق قول (( الأعلم » و « ابن مالك»، أنه ليس بصفة، بل عَلَم. ويبنى على علميته أنه في البسملة ونحوها بدل لا نعت، وأن الرحيم بـعـده نـعـت لـه لا نعت لاسم الله تعالى، إذ لا يتقدم البدل على النعت قاله شيخ الإسلام «زكريا الأنصاري»، وكذا قال الإمام القاضي ( البيضاوي و الملاخسرو وفي الدرر ) . الرحمن الرحيم، اسمان بنيا للمبالغة، وقال ( ابن المبارك » : الرحمن إذا سئل أعطى، والرحيم إذا لم يُسأل يغضب.
It has not been issued in His name, Exalted is He. If the ba is made transitive, it would be less far-fetched, for the meaning is: I have presented His name, Exalted, upon the intended object. If you say: How can the name be added to Allah, when Allah is the name, because the name and the named are one thing according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah? I say: It is said that 'name' here means naming, which is the utterance of the name, so the estimation is: By the remembrance of Allah I begin. And it is said that it is redundant, as in the saying: 'A caller calling him by the name of water,' meaning calling him by water. Then the estimation would be: By Allah I begin, and the mention of the name is to repel the suspicion of an oath. Allah: a name for the Essence that necessarily exists, deserving all praises, worshipped with right, having no derivation, and He is too great to have a derivation mentioned for Him. This is the choice of the Greatest Imam (Abu Hanifa) and al-Khalil, may Allah have mercy on them. The Creator alone is unique in it, not shared with anyone. He said: 'Do you know any equal to His name?' (Maryam 19:65) meaning: Do you know anyone named by this name other than Him. Its origin is 'ilah' (god), so the hamza was deleted contrary to analogy, and the definite article was substituted and became necessary, and it was stripped of the meaning of definiteness, and one of the two lams was assimilated into the other. Therefore it is said in the vocative: 'Ya Allah' with a cutoff, and it is said by analogy with a lightened hamza. Thus the assimilation and substitution are among the peculiarities of the majestic name, so that it is distinguished from others by the distinction of its named from others, with perfections not found in others. It is the most known of known things. Al-Rahman al-Rahim: two attributes derived from mercy. They differed whether they are the same meaning or different. Some said they are the same meaning, like nadman and nadim. Their meaning is 'possessor of mercy'; mentioning one after the other is for confirmation to reassure the hearts of the desirous, even though the first is not used except for the Creator. Al-Mubarrad said: It is bounty after bounty, and favor after favor. Some said there is a difference. Al-Rahman means generality: its meaning is the one who is compassionate to all His creation by providing for them in the world, not increasing the provision of the pious for their piety, nor decreasing the provision of the wicked for their wickedness. Al-Rahim means the one who rewards in the Hereafter, and forgiveness in the Hereafter is specific to the believers. Hence it is said in supplication: O Rahman of the world and the Hereafter. Thus in Ma'alim al-Tanzil. And in al-Kashshaf: In al-Rahman there is an intensity that is not in al-Rahim, i.e., because increased form usually indicates increased meaning, like 'qata'a' and 'qatta'a'. Thus, on this basis, the generality of al-Rahman is due to its not being specific to one of the two abodes, and the specificity of al-Rahim is due to its being specific to this world, contrary to what was mentioned in Ma'alim al-Tanzil, for there the generality of al-Rahman is due to its not being specific to some creatures (1) and they are the followers of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He said: 'A group of my ummah will continue to prevail over the truth, not harmed by those who oppose them, until the command of Allah comes while they are upon that.' The hadith was narrated by Muslim in the Book of Leadership, chapter: His statement: 'A group of my ummah will continue to prevail over the truth' (1920). (1) without some, and the specificity of al-Rahim is due to its being specific to some creatures, specifically the believers. And mercy: tenderness of heart, which is a psychological quality impossible for Him, Exalted. So it is either a transferred metaphor of beneficence, then it is an attribute of action, or of His will, then it is an attribute of essence. Or it is a representative metaphor: likening His action, Exalted, to the state of a king who is kind to his subjects, and he is tender to them, so he includes them in his good deeds, so the name is applied to him, and its ultimate meaning is intended: action or will, not its origin which is passion. So it is a representative metaphor. And the descriptive adjective is derived from 'rahima' while it is transitive, by making it intransitive, or by transferring it to a verb with damm. All this is based on the fact that al-Rahman is an attribute, and it is so originally, but it became a proper noun by predominance. Ibn Hisham said: The truth is the statement of al-A'lam and Ibn Malik that it is not an attribute but a proper noun. And it is built on its being a proper noun that in the basmalah and similar it is a substitute, not a modifier, and that al-Rahim after it is a modifier for it, not a modifier for the name of Allah, since the substitute does not precede the modifier, said by Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari, and likewise said by the Imam al-Qadi al-Baydawi and Mulla Khusraw and in al-Durar. Al-Rahman al-Rahim: two names built for intensity. And Ibn al-Mubarak said: Al-Rahman: if asked, He gives; al-Rahim: if not asked, He becomes angry.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic and theological commentary on the basmalah. It begins by addressing the grammatical construction of 'Bismillah', discussing whether the ba is for transitivity. It then tackles the issue of whether 'ism' (name) and 'musamma' (named) are identical, affirming the Ahl al-Sunnah position that they are the same. The name 'Allah' is analyzed etymologically, emphasizing it has no derivation and is unique to the divine essence, a view attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and al-Khalil. A Quranic verse (19:65) is cited to prove exclusivity. The derivation from 'ilah' is explained, with changes like hamza deletion and lam assimilation. The attributes al-Rahman and al-Rahim are then examined: whether they are synonymous or distinct. Two opinions are presented: one holds they are identical; another (from al-Kashshaf) distinguishes them by intensity and scope. The commentary favors that al-Rahman indicates universal mercy (general provision in this world) while al-Rahim indicates specific mercy (forgiveness in the Hereafter for believers). Mercy as a divine attribute is analyzed as metaphorically representing beneficence or will, not an emotional quality. Finally, it notes that al-Rahman has become a proper noun by predominance, supported by Ibn Hisham and others, affecting its grammatical role in the basmalah. The footnote mentions the Ahl al-Sunnah (Ash'ari and Maturidi) and a hadith on the steadfast group.

Key Terms
اسم (ism): In this passage, 'ism' is discussed as either the utterance (tasmia) or as the named entity (musamma). The author adopts the view that name and named are one.
تسمية (tasmia): The act of uttering the name, i.e., saying 'Bismillah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the word 'ism' in 'Bismillah' considered ambiguous?
A) Because it can be grammatically removed without changing the meaning.
B) Because it is a proof that the name and named are separate.
C) Because it might be interpreted as an oath.
D) Because it is derived from a different root.

Key Takeaway
In the basmalah, 'ism' is either the utterance of the name or redundant, and the name and named are identical according to Ahl al-Sunnah.

Introduction and Front Matter
advanced
10 min
The Divine Name 'Allah': Etymology and Uniqueness
Linguistic and theological analysis of the name 'Allah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic derivation of 'Allah' and explain why the Hanafi school asserts it has no etymology and is unique.

Core Idea
The name 'Allah' is a proper name for the Divine Essence, with no derivation, unique to Him, and unlike any other name.

The name 'Allah' is the most sacred word in Islam. This lesson delves into its linguistic origin, the debate over whether it is derived, and the scholarly consensus that it is a unique proper name for the Creator. The passage cites a Quranic verse to prove its exclusivity and explores its morphological peculiarities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'Allah'
The passage defines 'Allah' as the name for the Essence that necessarily exists, deserving all praises, worshipped with right, having no derivation.
This is the choice of Imam Abu Hanifa and al-Khalil.
2
Note the Quranic proof for uniqueness
The verse 'Do you know any equal to His name?' (Maryam 19:65) is cited to show that no one else shares this name.
The verse implies the name is exclusive to Allah.
3
Trace the proposed derivation from 'ilah'
The origin is said to be 'ilah' (god). The hamza was deleted, the definite article substituted, and one lam assimilated, creating the form 'Allah'.
These changes are contrary to normal analogy and are unique to this name.
4
Understand the vocative form 'Ya Allah'
The assimilation and substitution result in specific rules for the vocative case, e.g., 'Ya Allah' with a cutoff instead of the usual ya Allah.
This distinguishes 'Allah' from other names.
5
Conclude that 'Allah' is the most definite definite
The name is 'the most known of known things' because its uniqueness and perfection are unparalleled.
This affirms the Hanafi position that it is a proper name, not derived.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic and theological commentary on the basmalah. It begins by addressing the grammatical construction of 'Bismillah', discussing whether the ba is for transitivity. It then tackles the issue of whether 'ism' (name) and 'musamma' (named) are identical, affirming the Ahl al-Sunnah position that they are the same. A Quranic verse (19:65) is cited to prove exclusivity. The derivation from 'ilah' is explained, with changes like hamza deletion and lam assimilation. The attributes al-Rahman and al-Rahim are then examined: whether they are synonymous or distinct. Two opinions are presented: one holds they are identical; another (from al-Kashshaf) distinguishes them by intensity and scope. The commentary favors that al-Rahman indicates universal mercy (general provision in this world) while al-Rahim indicates specific mercy (forgiveness in the Hereafter for believers). Mercy as a divine attribute is analyzed as metaphorically representing beneficence or will, not an emotional quality. The footnote mentions the Ahl al-Sunnah (Ash'ari and Maturidi) and a hadith on the steadfast group.

Key Terms
الله (Allah): The proper name for the divine essence, necessarily existent, deserving praise, with no derivation. It is unique and not shared by any other.
اشتقاق (ishtiqaq): Derivation; the process of forming a word from a root. The name 'Allah' has no derivation.

Introduction and Front Matter
advanced
12 min
Al-Rahman and Al-Rahim: Mercy Attributes
Linguistic and theological analysis of al-Rahman and al-Rahim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between al-Rahman and al-Rahim according to the Hanafi school and identify the metaphor used for divine mercy.

Core Idea
Al-Rahman indicates general mercy for all creation, while al-Rahim indicates specific mercy for believers in the Hereafter.

Two of the most beautiful names of Allah are al-Rahman and al-Rahim. This lesson explores whether they are synonymous or distinct. According to the preferred view, al-Rahman refers to Allah's general mercy encompassing all creatures in this world, while al-Rahim refers to His specific mercy reserved for believers in the Hereafter. The commentary also addresses the metaphor of mercy as an attribute of action or essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on synonymy
Are al-Rahman and al-Rahim synonyms or do they have different meanings? The passage presents two opinions: some say they are identical (like nadman and nadim), while others say there is a difference.
The difference is important for theology.
2
Understand the preferred distinction in Ma'alim al-Tanzil
According to Ma'alim al-Tanzil, al-Rahman means general mercy in this world (providing for all), and al-Rahim means forgiveness in the Hereafter (specific to believers).
This is the view mentioned first.
3
Analyze the alternative view from al-Kashshaf
Al-Kashshaf adds that al-Rahman has intensity (mubalaghah) not in al-Rahim, and its generality is across both abodes, not just this world, while al-Rahim is specific to this world. This differs from Ma'alim al-Tanzil.
The increased form (fa'lan vs fa'il) indicates increased meaning.
4
Examine the metaphor of mercy
Mercy (rahmah) originally means tenderness of heart, which is impossible for Allah. Therefore, it is either a transferred metaphor for beneficence (attribute of action) or for His will (attribute of essence).
This preserves divine transcendence.
5
Note the grammatical status of al-Rahman
Originally an attribute, al-Rahman became a proper noun by predominance. In the basmalah, it functions as a substitute (badal) for 'Allah', not as a modifier (na't).
This is supported by Ibn Hisham and others.
